reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn


class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Stein Variational Gradient Descent (SVGD) update step.
    Maintains a set of particles approximating a posterior and updates
    them via a kernel-smoothed gradient that balances fitting the target
    with repulsion between particles.  The RBF kernel bandwidth is set
    via the median heuristic.
    """

    def __init__(self, dim):
        super().__init__()
        self.dim = dim
        # Target: N(0, I) for benchmarking
        self.register_buffer("target_mean", torch.zeros(dim))

    def _score_fn(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        """Gradient of log p(x) for N(0, I)."""
        return -x

    def forward(self, particles: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        """
        Args:
            particles: (N, dim) – current particle positions
        Returns:
            phi: (N, dim) – SVGD update direction
        """
        N = particles.shape[0]
        score = self._score_fn(particles)  # (N, dim)

        # Pairwise squared distances
        diff = particles.unsqueeze(0) - particles.unsqueeze(1)  # (N, N, dim)
        dist_sq = (diff ** 2).sum(-1)  # (N, N)

        # Median heuristic for bandwidth
        median_sq = torch.median(dist_sq.view(-1)).clamp(min=1e-5)
        h = median_sq / max(torch.log(torch.tensor(float(N))).item(), 1.0)

        K = torch.exp(-dist_sq / (2.0 * h))  # (N, N)
        grad_K = -diff / h * K.unsqueeze(-1)  # (N, N, dim)

        # SVGD update: phi(x_i) = (1/N) sum_j [ K(x_j, x_i) * score(x_j) + grad_K(x_j, x_i) ]
        phi = (K @ score + grad_K.sum(dim=0)) / N
        return phi


dim = 32
num_particles = 1024


def get_inputs():
    return [torch.randn(num_particles, dim)]


def get_init_inputs():
    return [dim]

reasoning
We need to write a custom CUDA kernel that computes the SVGD update for a set of particles. The reference model is fairly simple: compute pairwise squared distances, median heuristic for bandwidth h, RBF kernel K, grad_K, then phi = (K @ score + grad_K.sum(dim=0)) / N. Score is -x. We need to produce a kernel in CUDA using PyTorch's cpp_extension.load_inline. The kernel must accept a tensor of shape (N, dim), compute phi of shape (N, dim). The precision is fp32. We need to be faster than PyTorch reference. Let's understand the operations: N = 1024, dim = 32. That's moderate size. PyTorch does a bunch of broadcasting and creates large intermediate tensors: diff is (N,N,dim) = (1024,1024,32) ~ 134M elements, each float32 is 512MB. That's huge, may be memory-bound. Our custom kernel can compute pairwise interactions on the fly without storing huge diff or K matrices. So there's a lot of opportunity. We must compute median of all pairwise squared distances. But since N=1024, N*N=1,048,576, which is manageable. However, in CUDA we could compute all dist_sq and then find median. But computing all pairwise squared distances explicitly is also O(N^2) memory and compute. For N=1024, we can compute dist_sq into a buffer of size N*N, then compute median using something like torch.median (or a custom kernel). But we want speed. The PyTorch reference computes diff (N,N,dim) which uses a lot of memory and compute. Better approach: compute pairwise squared distances efficiently, perhaps using matrix multiplication: dist_sq = ||x_i||^2 + ||x_j||^2 - 2 x_i·x_j. That requires (N,dim) input, compute norms (N), then dist_sq = norms_i + norms_j^T - 2 * x @ x.T. This is standard and avoids storing diff. Then compute median of dist_sq (flattened). Then compute K and grad_K on the fly in a kernel. Since N is only 1024, we could also compute everything in one kernel: each thread block computes a tile of output phi for a subset of i particles, iterating over j. For each j, compute diff = x_j - x_i, dist_sq = dot(diff, diff). Compute K = exp(-dist_sq / (2h)). Compute term = K * score_j + (-diff/h) * K. Accumulate sum. At the end divide by N. This single kernel avoids storing K and grad_K completely. It also avoids the large diff tensor. The only challenge is computing the median of dist_sq. But we can compute dist_sq using the matmul approach, store it in a N*N temporary (1M floats = 4MB), find median on GPU or CPU. 4MB is tiny. So we can compute dist_sq via efficient cuBLAS (torch.mm). Actually we can also compute dist_sq in the same kernel by first computing pairwise distances fully, but that requires materializing them or doing two passes. Two passes: first compute dist_sq matrix, find median. Second pass: compute phi with the median. Simplify: Use PyTorch operations to compute dist_sq efficiently: norms = (particles**2).sum(dim=1, keepdim=True) -> (N,1). dist_sq = norms + norms.t() - 2 * particles @ particles.t(). This yields (N,N). Then median = dist_sq.view(-1).median().clamp(min=1e-5). This will be fast and use only O(N^2) memory for dist_sq (4MB). The heavy diff tensor (512MB) is avoided. Then we can compute phi using a custom kernel that takes particles, score (=-particles), h, and output phi. That's possible. But maybe even the phi computation can be done efficiently in PyTorch without custom kernel? Let's see. Phi = (K @ score + grad_K.sum(dim=0)) / N. K = exp(-dist_sq/(2h)). That's (N,N). K @ score is (N,N) @ (N,dim) = (N,dim). grad_K = -diff/h * K.unsqueeze(-1). diff = particles_i - particles_j? Wait diff in reference is particles.unsqueeze(0) - particles.unsqueeze(1) = x_j? Actually unsqueeze(0) is (1,N,dim), unsqueeze(1) is (N,1,dim). diff = particles[0,j] - particles[i,0]? Let's compute: diff_{i,j} = particles_j - particles_i? Wait (1,N,dim) - (N,1,dim) with broadcasting: result shape (N,N,dim), element (i,j,d) = particles[0,j,d] - particles[i,0,d] = x_j - x_i. But later they compute grad_K = -diff / h * K.unsqueeze(-1). And phi = (K @ score + grad_K.sum(dim=0)) / N. Let's examine the formula: SVGD phi(x_i) = (1/N) sum_j [ K(x_j, x_i) * score(x_j) + grad_K(x_j, x_i) ]. Typically grad_K(x_j, x_i) = ∇_{x_i} K(x_j, x_i). For RBF K(x_j, x_i) = exp(-||x_j - x_i||^2/(2h)). Then ∇_{x_i} K = -(x_i - x_j)/h * K = (x_j - x_i)/h * K. Wait reference uses grad_K = -diff/h * K, where diff = x_j - x_i. So grad_K = -(x_j - x_i)/h * K = (x_i - x_j)/h * K. That matches ∇_{x_i} K = -(x_j - x_i)/h * K? Actually derivative w.r.t x_i of -||x_j - x_i||^2 is 2(x_j - x_i). Let's compute: K = exp(-||x_j - x_i||^2/(2h)). ∂K/∂x_i = exp(...)*(-1/(2h))*2*(x_i - x_j) = -(x_i - x_j)/h * K = (x_j - x_i)/h * K. So grad_K should be (x_j - x_i)/h * K. But reference says grad_K = -diff/h * K, and diff = x_j - x_i. So grad_K = -(x_j - x_i)/h * K = (x_i - x_j)/h * K. That seems off by a sign? Let's check the reference code carefully. diff = particles.unsqueeze(0) - particles.unsqueeze(1) # particles.unsqueeze(0) shape (1,N,dim) # particles.unsqueeze(1) shape (N,1,dim) # Broadcasting: diff[i,j] = particles[0,j] - particles[i,0] = x_j - x_i. grad_K = -diff / h * K.unsqueeze(-1) = -(x_j - x_i)/h * K = (x_i - x_j)/h * K. But the SVGD formula uses grad_K(x_j, x_i) = ∇_{x_i} K(x_j, x_i). That should be (x_i - x_j)/h * K? Let's compute derivative w.r.t x_i of r = x_j - x_i. Wait the argument is -||r||^2/(2h) where r = x_j - x_i. ∂/∂x_i (-||r||^2) = -2 * r * ∂r/∂x_i = -2 * r * (-1) = 2r. So ∂K/∂x_i = K * (1/(2h)) * 2r = r/h * K = (x_j - x_i)/h * K. Wait this is positive. Let's recompute: K = exp(-||x_j - x_i||^2/(2h)). Let f = -||x_j - x_i||^2/(2h). ∂f/∂x_i = -(1/(2h)) * 2 * (x_i - x_j) = -(x_i - x_j)/h = (x_j - x_i)/h. So ∂K/∂x_i = K * (x_j - x_i)/h. So grad_K = (x_j - x_i)/h * K. But reference has grad_K = -diff/h * K = -(x_j - x_i)/h * K. That is the negative of the correct derivative? Let's double-check diff. Maybe unsqueeze(0) - unsqueeze(1) yields diff[i,j] = particles[i] - particles[j]? Let's verify. particles shape (N,dim). particles.unsqueeze(0) shape (1,N,dim). particles.unsqueeze(1) shape (N,1,dim). When broadcasting subtract, PyTorch aligns from trailing dimensions: - Rightmost: dim matches. - Middle: N vs 1 -> broadcast to N. - Leftmost: 1 vs N -> broadcast to N. So shape is (N,N,dim). Element (i,j,d) = particles[0,j,d] - particles[i,0,d] = x_j - x_i. Yes, diff[i,j] = x_j - x_i. Then grad_K = -diff/h * K = -(x_j - x_i)/h * K = (x_i - x_j)/h * K. But SVGD formula phi(x_i) = (1/N) sum_j [ K(x_j, x_i) * score(x_j) + ∇_{x_i} K(x_j, x_i) ]. ∇_{x_i} K = (x_j - x_i)/h * K (as derived). But reference uses negative of that. Wait maybe the formula they implement is phi(x_i) = (1/N) sum_j [ K(x_j, x_i) * score(x_j) + ∇_{x_j} K(x_j, x_i) ]? Let's check the original SVGD paper: phi(x) = (1/n) sum_i [ k(x_i, x) ∇_{x_i} log p(x_i) + ∇_{x_i} k(x_i, x) ]. Here first argument is x_i, second is x. So ∇_{x_i} k(x_i, x) = derivative w.r.t first argument. If we set x = x_i (target), x_i = x_j (source), then ∇_{x_j} k(x_j, x_i). k(x_j, x_i) = exp(-||x_j - x_i||^2/(2h)). ∂/∂x_j k = -(x_j - x_i)/h * k. That matches reference: grad_K = -diff/h * K where diff = x_j - x_i. So reference's grad_K is derivative w.r.t the first argument (the source particle x_j). And the sum is over j of that. In our kernel, we have `if (tid < dim) { ... } else { sdata[tid] = 0; } __syncthreads();`. To avoid using `if (tid < dim)` before syncthreads, we can write: ``` float diff_reg = 0.0f; float sq = 0.0f; if (tid < dim) { float xi = particles[i*dim + tid]; float xj = particles[j*dim + tid]; diff_reg = xj - xi; sq = diff_reg * diff_reg; } sdata[tid] = sq; __syncthreads(); ``` This ensures all threads (including tid >= dim) write to sdata. Good. Then reduction: ``` for (int s = blockSize/2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (tid < s) sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + s]; __syncthreads(); } ``` All threads participate? The condition `tid < s` means some threads (tid >= s) don't do anything, but they still reach __syncthreads(). In CUDA, all threads in the block must reach __syncthreads(). Since the __syncthreads() is outside the if, all threads reach it. Actually inside the for loop, the __syncthreads() is after the if. So all threads (tid >= s) skip the add but still hit __syncthreads(). This is valid because the branch is uniform? No, it's divergent, but __syncthreads() inside a branch is allowed only if all threads in the block take the same path or if the __syncthreads() is executed by all threads in the block regardless of path. In our code: ``` for (...) { if (tid < s) { sdata[tid] += sdata[tid+s]; } __syncthreads(); } ``` At each iteration, threads with tid >= s do not execute the add, but they all execute __syncthreads(). This is valid because __syncthreads() is reachable by all threads in the block (the if doesn't guard the syncthreads). Actually the CUDA Programming Guide says: `__syncthreads()` is allowed in conditional code only if the conditional evaluates identically across the entire thread block. Here the condition `tid < s` is not identical across threads, but __syncthreads() is outside the conditional (it's after the if statement). So all threads will execute __syncthreads(). This is allowed. Good. After reduction, `float dist_sq = sdata[0];`. Since all threads need K, they can just read sdata[0]. The kernel: ``` __global__ void compute_dist_sq(const float* particles, int N, int dim, float* dist_sq) { int i = blockIdx.x; int j = threadIdx.x + blockIdx.y * blockDim.x; if (i >= N || j >= N) return; float sum = 0.0f; const float* xi = particles + i * dim; const float* xj = particles + j * dim; for (int d = 0; d < dim; ++d) { float diff = xj[d] - xi[d]; sum += diff * diff; } dist_sq[i * N + j] = sum; } ``` This kernel doesn't need shared memory. It uses 1 thread per (i,j) pair. For N=1024, we can launch grid (N, (N+255)/256), block 256. This is very simple and exact. But wait: diff = xj - xi, same as reference. sum of squares. exact. Now, let's write the code. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import math from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_src = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> __global__ void compute_dist_sq_kernel(const float* particles, int N, int dim, float* dist_sq) { int i = blockIdx.x; int j = threadIdx.x + blockIdx.y * blockDim.x; if (i >= N || j >= N) return; float sum = 0.0f; const float* xi = particles + i * dim; const float* xj = particles + j * dim; for (int d = 0; d < dim; ++d) { float diff = xj[d] - xi[d]; extern __shared__ float sdata[]; float inv_h = 1.0f / h; float inv_2h = 0.5f * inv_h; float inv_N = 1.0f / (float)N; float accum = 0.0f; float diff_reg = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { float sq = 0.0f; if (tid < dim) { float xi = particles[i * dim + tid]; float xj = particles[j * dim + tid]; diff_reg = xj - xi; sq = diff_reg * diff_reg; } sdata[tid] = sq; __syncthreads(); // tree reduction for (int s = blockSize / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (tid < s) { sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + s]; } __syncthreads(); } float dist_sq = sdata[0]; float K = expf(-dist_sq * inv_2h); if (tid < dim) { float term = K * (-particles[j * dim + tid] + diff_reg * inv_h); accum += term; } __syncthreads(); } if (tid < dim) { out[i * dim + tid] = accum * inv_N; } } torch::Tensor forward_svgd(torch::Tensor particles) { int N = particles.size(0); int dim = particles.size(1); auto options = torch::TensorOptions().dtype(torch::kFloat32).device(particles.device()); // Compute pairwise squared distances auto dist_sq = torch::empty({N, N}, options); dim3 block(256); dim3 grid(N, (N + block.x - 1) / block.x); compute_dist_sq_kernel<<<grid, block>>>( particles.data_ptr<float>(), N, dim, dist_sq.data_ptr<float>()); // Median heuristic for bandwidth float median_sq = dist_sq.view(-1).median().item<float>(); if (median_sq < 1e-5f) median_sq = 1e-5f; } svgd_phi_kernel<<<N, threads, threads * sizeof(float)>>>( particles.data_ptr<float>(), h, N, dim, out.data_ptr<float>()); return out; } ''' # Load inline svgd_cuda = load_inline( name="svgd_cuda", cpp_sources="", cuda_sources=cuda_src, functions=["forward_svgd"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3"], verbose=False, ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, dim): super().__init__() self.dim = dim self.register_buffer("target_mean", torch.zeros(dim)) def forward(self, particles: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor: return svgd_cuda.forward_svgd(particles) ``` Wait, there are a few issues: 1. `cpp_sources` cannot contain a `PYBIND11_MODULE`. Let's rewrite the dist_sq kernel for better performance: ``` __global__ void compute_dist_sq_kernel(const float* particles, int N, int dim, float* dist_sq) { int i = blockIdx.x; int j = threadIdx.x + blockIdx.y * blockDim.x; if (i >= N || j >= N) return; extern __shared__ float s_xi[]; if (threadIdx.x < dim) { s_xi[threadIdx.x] = particles[i * dim + threadIdx.x]; } __syncthreads(); float sum = 0.0f; const float* xj = particles + j * dim; for (int d = 0; d < dim; ++d) { float diff = xj[d] - s_xi[d]; sum += diff * diff; } dist_sq[i * N + j] = sum; } ``` Shared mem size = dim * sizeof(float) = 128 bytes. Let's keep it simple: no shared memory for dist_sq kernel. Just loop and read from global memory. L1/L2 broadcast is good enough. ``` __global__ void compute_dist_sq_kernel(const float* particles, int N, int dim, float* dist_sq) { int i = blockIdx.x; int j = threadIdx.x + blockIdx.y * blockDim.x; if (i >= N || j >= N) return; float sum = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < dim; ++d) { float diff = particles[j * dim + d] - particles[i * dim + d]; sum += diff * diff; } dist_sq[i * N + j] = sum; } ``` This is simple. For i fixed, all threads read particles[i*dim + d] from the same address. The hardware broadcasts this to all threads. Similarly for each j, threads in a warp may read different j's, so the reads for xj are coalesced across j but not across d. Wait, in this kernel, each thread handles a different (i,j) pair. For a fixed block (i is fixed), threads have different j. They all execute the loop over d together. In iteration d, thread for j reads `particles[j*dim + d]`. Since j varies across threads, the addresses are `particles + j*dim + d`. For consecutive j, addresses are separated by dim=32 floats = 128 bytes. This is a strided access with stride 128 bytes. For a warp of 32 threads, the addresses are 128 bytes apart. A memory transaction on modern GPUs is 32 bytes (or 128 bytes). With a stride of 128 bytes, each thread reads a different 128-byte segment. The warp will generate 32 transactions of 128 bytes each, reading 4KB total, but only using 128 bytes (32 floats). That's terrible memory bandwidth! Because the warp is reading scattered memory. So this kernel is actually very inefficient for memory access. Each warp reads one element per thread, but they are strided by dim (32) floats = 128 bytes. This means 32 separate cache lines per warp. That's bad. To fix this, we need to transpose the access or change the launch geometry. Better: have each block handle a tile of (i,j) pairs, and load a tile of particles into shared memory. Or change the loop order: each thread computes one pair and loops over d. The memory access pattern for x_j is strided by j. But we can restructure: Instead of having each block handle a single i and threads handle j, we can have each block handle a tile of i and j, and each thread compute one (i,j) pair. But the memory pattern for reading particles is still strided. Alternatively, we can have each block handle one i and have threads loop over j in a strided way? Actually the fundamental issue is that for a fixed d, the array is laid out as (N,dim). So particles[j*dim + d] for varying j are separated by dim. This is a strided access. But it's coalesced only if consecutive threads read consecutive j. For a warp of 32 threads, j values are j0, j0+1, ..., j0+31. The addresses are base + (j0 + tid)*dim + d. The stride between consecutive threads is dim floats = 128 bytes. On A100, global memory load for a warp can coalesce multiple addresses into fewer transactions if they fall within the same cache line segment (128 bytes). But with stride 128 bytes, each address is in a different 128-byte segment. So each thread generates a separate 128-byte transaction. Memory bandwidth utilization: reading 128 bytes to get 4 bytes, i.e., 3.125% utilization. That's terrible. However, L2 cache may hold the entire particles array (128KB). L2 bandwidth is high. But still, 32 transactions per warp is a lot. To improve, we can read particles in a transposed manner or load a block of particles into shared memory. For example, a block of 32x32 threads could load a tile of 32 particles into shared memory, then compute distances among them. But that complicates the kernel. But for N=1024, dim=32, total memory reads in dist_sq kernel are 2 * N * N * dim floats = 64M floats = 256MB (since each pair reads two particles). At 1 TB/s, 256MB takes 0.256 ms. With poor coalescing, effective bandwidth drops to, say, 100 GB/s (L2), taking 2.5 ms. That's still not huge, but might be slower than the matmul. The matmul `particles @ particles.t()` reads each particle multiple times but in a highly coalesced manner via Tensor Cores or optimized shared memory tiling. It will be much faster than the naive custom kernel. For the phi kernel, the memory access pattern is different: block i has threads 0..31 (for dim). They loop over j. For each j, thread tid reads `particles[j*dim + tid]`. Since tid varies 0..31, addresses are `base + j*dim + tid`. Consecutive threads read consecutive floats. This is perfectly coalesced! Good. And they read `particles[i*dim + tid]` also coalesced (but same for all j). So phi kernel memory access is great. Thus, the custom dist_sq kernel has bad memory access. To fix this, we should compute dist_sq using the PyTorch matmul (or an efficient custom kernel). The PyTorch matmul has excellent memory access. Alternatively, we can compute dist_sq by launching a kernel where each block handles one i, and threads within the block cooperate to read a block of j's particles into shared memory. But since dim=32, we can have each block read, say, 32 particles at a time. Another efficient way: use the matmul approach but in CUDA: dist_sq = norms + norms^T - 2 * (particles @ particles^T). We can compute `particles @ particles^T` with a single line of PyTorch, which calls cuBLAS. That's the best. So we should use PyTorch for `dist_sq`. Given that, let's compute `dist_sq` in PyTorch: ``` particles_t = particles.t() # (dim, N) prod = particles @ particles_t # (N,N) norms = (particles ** 2).sum(dim=1, keepdim=True) # (N,1) dist_sq = norms + norms.t() - 2.0 * prod dist_sq = dist_sq.clamp(min=0.0) ``` Will this match reference exactly? Let's design it: - Grid: (N, num_tiles_j) where num_tiles_j = (N + BJ - 1) / BJ. - Block: (dim, BJ) = (32, 32). 1024 threads per block. - For block (i, tile_j): j_start = tile_j * BJ. local_j = threadIdx.y (0..31). j = j_start + local_j. d = threadIdx.x (0..31). This kernel has perfect coalescing for loading particles: for a fixed j, threads d=0..31 read consecutive addresses. Since there are 32 j's per block, we load 32 particles in parallel. For each j, a warp reads its particle. Let's write this kernel. ``` __global__ void compute_dist_sq_tiled(const float* particles, int N, int dim, float* dist_sq) { int i = blockIdx.x; int tile_j = blockIdx.y; int d = threadIdx.x; int local_j = threadIdx.y; int BJ = blockDim.y; int j = tile_j * BJ + local_j; __shared__ float s_xi[32]; // assume dim <= 32 if (local_j == 0) { s_xi[d] = particles[i * dim + d]; } __syncthreads(); float xj = 0.0f; if (j < N) { xj = particles[j * dim + d]; } float diff = xj - s_xi[d]; float sq = diff * diff; // warp reduce sq across d (first dimension) for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sq, offset); } if (d == 0 && j < N) { dist_sq[i * N + j] = sq; } } ``` This kernel assumes dim <= 32. Let's write the phi kernel as: ``` __global__ void svgd_phi_kernel(const float* particles, float h, int N, int dim, float* out) { int i = blockIdx.x; int d = threadIdx.x; float xi = particles[i * dim + d]; float accum = 0.0f; float inv_h = 1.0f / h; float inv_2h = 0.5f * inv_h; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { float xj = particles[j * dim + d]; float diff = xj - xi; // reduce diff^2 across warp float sq = diff * diff; for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sq, offset); } float dist_sq = sq; // only correct for lane 0, but we need all lanes to have it dist_sq = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, dist_sq, 0); float K = expf(-dist_sq * inv_2h); float term = K * (-xj + diff * inv_h); accum += term; } out[i * dim + d] = accum / (float)N; } ``` Wait, does `__shfl_down_sync` work when `d` varies 0..31? Yes, because all threads in the block are in one warp (block size = 32). The `__shfl_down_sync` with mask 0xffffffff works. After the loop, lane 0 has the sum. Then `__shfl_sync(0xffffffff, dist_sq, 0)` broadcasts it to all lanes. All lanes then compute K and term. Since there is no divergence, it's perfect. But what if dim is not exactly 32? Python fallback: ``` def forward(self, particles): N = particles.shape[0] dim = particles.shape[1] if dim > 1024: # fallback score = -particles diff = particles.unsqueeze(0) - particles.unsqueeze(1) dist_sq = (diff ** 2).sum(-1) median_sq = dist_sq.view(-1).median().clamp(min=1e-5) h = median_sq.item() / max(math.log(float(N)), 1.0) K = torch.exp(-dist_sq / (2.0 * h)) grad_K = -diff / h * K.unsqueeze(-1) phi = (K @ score + grad_K.sum(dim=0)) / N return phi else: return svgd_cuda.forward_svgd(particles) ``` But the fallback is exactly the reference, so it's correct. Alternatively, we can compute the fallback efficiently in Python using the matmul formulation: ``` score = -particles norms = (particles ** 2).sum(dim=1, keepdim=True) dist_sq = norms + norms.t() - 2.0 * particles @ particles.t() median_sq = dist_sq.view(-1).median().clamp(min=1e-5) h = median_sq.item() / max(math.log(float(N)), 1.0) K = torch.exp(-dist_sq / (2.0 * h)) phi = (K.matmul(score) + (K.matmul(particles) - particles * K.sum(dim=1, keepdim=True)) / h) / N ``` This avoids huge diff. We can write: ``` __global__ void compute_dist_sq_kernel(const float* particles, int N, int dim, float* dist_sq) { int i = blockIdx.x; int j = threadIdx.x + blockIdx.y * blockDim.x; if (i >= N || j >= N) return; float sum = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < dim; ++d) { float diff = particles[j * dim + d] - particles[i * dim + d]; sum += diff * diff; } dist_sq[i * N + j] = sum; } ``` Despite strided memory access, it's simple and correct. The performance may not be optimal, but N is only 1024. Let's estimate worst-case time: 1M threads, each reading dim=32 floats from two arrays with stride 128 bytes. Effective memory bandwidth maybe 50-100 GB/s. Total reads: 2 * 1M * 32 * 4 = 256MB. At 50 GB/s, that's 5ms. At 100 GB/s, 2.5ms. That might be a bit high. But L2 cache might improve it because the data is small (128KB). When reading `particles[i*dim + d]` for fixed i, all threads read the same address. L2 handles this. When reading `particles[j*dim + d]`, each thread reads a different address. Since there are 1M threads but only 1024*32=32768 unique floats, each float is read many times (by N threads for each i). The L2 cache will hold all particles. So after the first reads, subsequent reads will hit L2. Actually, each block processes a fixed i. For a given j, that particle is read by all blocks (all i). So each x_j is read N times. Since N=1024, total reads of each x_j = 1024. The L2 cache can hold all 1024 particles. So after the first few blocks, all particles are in L2. Then each block reads x_j from L2. L2 bandwidth is much higher than global memory, maybe 2-5 TB/s. So the strided access from L2 is fast. The first reads from global memory are also not terrible. So total time might be well under 1ms. Let's not overthink dist_sq. Use the simple kernel or PyTorch matmul. Let's use the simple custom kernel to guarantee exactness. Actually, let's just use PyTorch chunked compute for dist_sq. It's exact and probably fast enough. For chunk=512: ``` dist_sq = torch.empty(N, N, device=particles.device, dtype=particles.dtype) chunk = 512 for i_start in range(0, N, chunk): i_end = min(i_start+chunk, N) diff = particles[i_start:i_end].unsqueeze(1) - particles.unsqueeze(0) dist_sq[i_start:i_end] = (diff ** 2).sum(-1) ``` This is 2 iterations. Each diff is (512, 1024, 32), 64MB. The compute is a subtraction, square, sum. PyTorch will fuse these into one kernel? Actually `diff ** 2` is one kernel, `.sum(-1)` is another. Or maybe fused? Let's do that: Python `forward`: ``` def forward(self, particles): N = particles.shape[0] # compute dist_sq in chunks dist_sq = torch.empty(N, N, device=particles.device, dtype=particles.dtype) chunk = 512 for i_start in range(0, N, chunk): i_end = min(i_start + chunk, N) diff = particles[i_start:i_end].unsqueeze(1) - particles.unsqueeze(0) dist_sq[i_start:i_end] = (diff ** 2).sum(-1) median_sq = dist_sq.view(-1).median().clamp(min=1e-5) h = median_sq.item() / max(math.log(float(N)), 1.0) return svgd_cuda.forward_svgd(particles, h) ``` And `forward_svgd` in C++ takes `particles` and `h`. This is great! It avoids C++ complexity for dist_sq, uses exact PyTorch chunked compute (fast), and the custom kernel is simpler. But wait, is the chunked compute fast enough? Let's think. For chunk=512, diff is (512, 1024, 32). That's 512*1024*32*4 = 67,108,864 bytes = 64MB. The subtraction kernel reads particles (32KB + 64KB) and writes 64MB. The square kernel reads 64MB and writes 64MB. The sum kernel reads 64MB and writes 2MB. For 2 chunks, total memory traffic: 2 * ( (~64MB read + 64MB write) * 2 + 64MB read + 2MB write ) ~ 2 * (256MB + 66MB) = ~644MB. At 1TB/s, that's <1ms. Overhead of 6 kernel launches is negligible (~6 us each). So total maybe <1ms. Good. Reference's single diff of 512MB would also have similar memory traffic but in one go. Actually, the reference's `particles.unsqueeze(0) - particles.unsqueeze(1)` creates a tensor of shape (1024,1024,32) = 134,217,728 elements = 134M floats = 512MB. That's a huge allocation. Modern GPUs have tens of GB of memory, so 512MB allocation is not a problem. But PyTorch's allocator overhead for 512MB might be non-trivial. Chunking into 64MB pieces reduces allocator overhead. Let's write the C++ wrapper. ``` torch::Tensor forward_svgd(torch::Tensor particles, float h) { int N = particles.size(0); int dim = particles.size(1); auto out = torch::empty_like(particles); int threads = 32; while (threads < dim) threads <<= 1; // We can cap at 1024 and fallback from Python size_t shared_mem = threads * sizeof(float); svgd_phi_kernel<<<N, threads, shared_mem>>>( particles.data_ptr<float>(), h, N, dim, out.data_ptr<float>()); return out; } ``` Wait, if dim > 1024, threads will exceed 1024 and CUDA launch will fail. We handle that in Python by not calling the custom kernel. But we also need to support dim not a power of two and less than 32. `threads` starts at 32. If dim=16, threads=32. That's fine. The kernel handles tid < dim. Let's verify the kernel with threads=32 and dim=16. Threads 16..31 will set `sq=0` and `diff_reg=0`. Reduction sums all 32 entries. `dist_sq` will be correct because entries 16..31 contribute 0. Then K is computed. Then threads 16..31 skip the accumulation because `tid < dim` is false. At the end, they skip writing output. This is correct. One issue: `diff_reg` for threads 16..31 is set to 0. In the term computation, they don't use it. Good. But in the loop over j, for each j, threads 16..31 set `sq=0` and `diff_reg=0`. Then syncthreads. Then reduction. Then they skip accumulation. Then syncthreads at the end. Since they always reach the syncthreads, it's fine. However, what if dim > threads? Our code ensures threads >= dim (since threads starts at 32 and doubles until >= dim). If dim > 1024, we fallback. So no issue. Now, there is a subtle bug in the reduction: for threads > dim, we set sdata[tid] = 0. The reduction sums all `threads` entries. For example, dim=16, threads=32. sdata[0..15] have sq values, sdata[16..31] have 0. Reduction over 32 entries yields correct sum. Good. Another subtlety: In the kernel, we use `particles.data_ptr<float>()`. We need to ensure tensor is contiguous and float. Since input is float32 and typical memory layout is contiguous, this is fine. We can add checks in the C++ wrapper if needed. But not required. Let's think about performance of the phi kernel. Each block loops over j from 0 to N-1 (1024). For each j: - Read x_i[d] and x_j[d] from global memory (coalesced). - Compute diff and sq. - Write to shared memory. - __syncthreads() - 5 rounds of reduction (for threads=32) with __syncthreads(). - Read sdata[0]. - expf. - Read x_j[d] again (coalesced). - Compute term and accum. - __syncthreads(). So per iteration, there are about 7 syncthreads(). For a single warp block, __syncthreads may be compiled as a memory fence because the block is one warp. But it's still some overhead. However, for N=1024, total iterations per block = 1024. So per block, 7*1024 = 7168 syncthreads. For 1024 blocks, 7.3M syncthreads. That's okay. Could we avoid the second __syncthreads() at the end of the j loop? Yes, if we don't use shared memory in the next iteration's read/write? Actually we do use shared memory at the start of the next iteration: `sdata[tid] = sq;`. This write could race with threads from the previous iteration still reading `sdata[0]`. But after `dist_sq = sdata[0];`, all threads read sdata[0]. Since it's a broadcast (same address for all threads), and there are no writes to sdata after the reduction until the next iteration's `sdata[tid] = sq;`, we need to ensure all threads have read sdata[0] before any thread writes sdata[tid]. In a single warp, all threads execute in lockstep, so by the time any thread reaches the next iteration's write, all threads have already read sdata[0] from the previous iteration. So we could omit the second __syncthreads() for blockSize <= 32. But for safety and generality (blockSize > 32, multiple warps), we need it. Some warps might finish the term computation and go to the next iteration's `sdata[tid] = sq;` while other warps are still reading `sdata[0]` from the previous iteration. So __syncthreads() is needed. But for blockSize=32, we can safely omit it because it's a single warp. However, the compiler might not know that blockSize is 32 at compile time. We could use `if (blockSize > 32) __syncthreads();`? But blockSize is not known at compile time? Actually it's a runtime variable, but the compiler may not optimize it. We can add `__syncthreads();` regardless; for a single warp, it's cheap. Actually, CUDA's `__syncthreads()` for a single warp does not require any synchronization (since warps are implicitly synchronized), but the instruction is still issued? According to some sources, `__syncthreads()` in a single-warp block has negligible overhead (just a few cycles). So we can keep it. Now, about reading x_j twice per iteration: once for diff, once for term. We can avoid the second read by storing `xj` in a register. In the kernel, after computing `diff` and `sq`, we store `sq` in shared memory. But we can keep `xj` and `diff` in registers. Since `diff_reg` is already stored. But the code currently recomputes `-particles[j*dim + tid]` in the term. We can change to use `xj` variable: ``` float xi = particles[i * dim + tid]; float xj = particles[j * dim + tid]; float diff_reg = xj - xi; sq = diff_reg * diff_reg; ... float term = K * (-xj + diff_reg * inv_h); ``` This avoids the second read. We already have `xj` in a local variable. So we just need to reuse it. In the current code, `if (tid < dim)` reads `xi` and `xj` into locals. `diff_reg` is local. After reduction, `if (tid < dim)` computes term using `particles[j*dim + tid]`. Let's reuse `xj`. Let's rewrite: ``` float accum = 0.0f; float diff_reg = 0.0f; float xj_reg = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { float sq = 0.0f; if (tid < dim) { float xi = particles[i * dim + tid]; xj_reg = particles[j * dim + tid]; diff_reg = xj_reg - xi; sq = diff_reg * diff_reg; } sdata[tid] = sq; __syncthreads(); for (int s = blockSize / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (tid < s) sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + s]; __syncthreads(); } float dist_sq = sdata[0]; float K = expf(-dist_sq * inv_2h); if (tid < dim) { float term = K * (-xj_reg + diff_reg * inv_h); accum += term; } __syncthreads(); } ``` Good. Now, one more improvement: we can load `xi` once per block, outside the j loop, into a register or shared memory. Currently each iteration loads `particles[i*dim + tid]`, which is the same address for all j. Since it's coalesced and in L1/L2, it's cheap. But we can hoist it out: ``` float xi = 0.0f; if (tid < dim) xi = particles[i * dim + tid]; ``` Then inside the loop: ``` xj_reg = particles[j * dim + tid]; diff_reg = xj_reg - xi; ``` This avoids reloading xi N times. For N=1024, that's 32KB saved. Not huge, but easy. Let's incorporate that. Also, `inv_h`, `inv_2h`, `inv_N` can be computed once outside loop. Let's finalize the kernel code. Let's draft the code. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import math from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline # CUDA source cuda_source = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> __global__ void svgd_phi_kernel(const float* particles, float h, int N, int dim, float* out) { int i = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int blockSize = blockDim.x; extern __shared__ float sdata[]; float inv_h = 1.0f / h; float inv_2h = 0.5f * inv_h; float inv_N = 1.0f / (float)N; float xi = 0.0f; if (tid < dim) { xi = particles[i * dim + tid]; } float accum = 0.0f; float diff_reg = 0.0f; float xj_reg = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { float sq = 0.0f; if (tid < dim) { xj_reg = particles[j * dim + tid]; diff_reg = xj_reg - xi; sq = diff_reg * diff_reg; } sdata[tid] = sq; __syncthreads(); for (int s = blockSize / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (tid < s) { sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + s]; } __syncthreads(); } float dist_sq = sdata[0]; float K = expf(-dist_sq * inv_2h); if (tid < dim) { float term = K * (-xj_reg + diff_reg * inv_h); accum += term; } __syncthreads(); } if (tid < dim) { out[i * dim + tid] = accum * inv_N; } } torch::Tensor forward_svgd(torch::Tensor particles, float h) { int N = particles.size(0); int dim = particles.size(1); auto out = torch::empty_like(particles); int threads = 32; while (threads < dim) threads <<= 1; // Guard: if dim too large, we shouldn't reach here because Python checks. // But cap at 1024 to be safe. if (threads > 1024) threads = 1024; size_t shared_mem = threads * sizeof(float); svgd_phi_kernel<<<N, threads, shared_mem>>>( particles.data_ptr<float>(), h, N, dim, out.data_ptr<float>()); return out; } ''' svgd_cuda = load_inline( name="svgd_cuda", cpp_sources="", cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=["forward_svgd"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3"], verbose=False, ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, dim): super().__init__() self.dim = dim self.register_buffer("target_mean", torch.zeros(dim)) def _score_fn(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor: return -x def forward(self, particles: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor: N = particles.shape[0] dim = particles.shape[1] # Fallback to PyTorch reference for very large dim (not expected in benchmark) if dim > 1024: score = -particles diff = particles.unsqueeze(0) - particles.unsqueeze(1) dist_sq = (diff ** 2).sum(-1) median_sq = dist_sq.view(-1).median().clamp(min=1e-5) h = median_sq.item() / max(math.log(float(N)), 1.0) K = torch.exp(-dist_sq / (2.0 * h)) grad_K = -diff / h * K.unsqueeze(-1) phi = (K.matmul(score) + grad_K.sum(dim=0)) / N return phi # Compute pairwise squared distances in chunks to avoid huge intermediate dist_sq = torch.empty(N, N, device=particles.device, dtype=particles.dtype) chunk = 512 for i_start in range(0, N, chunk): i_end = min(i_start + chunk, N) diff = particles[i_start:i_end].unsqueeze(1) - particles.unsqueeze(0) dist_sq[i_start:i_end] = (diff ** 2).sum(-1) median_sq = dist_sq.view(-1).median().clamp(min=1e-5) h = median_sq.item() / max(math.log(float(N)), 1.0) return svgd_cuda.forward_svgd(particles, h) ``` Let's double-check the fallback and chunk logic. In the fallback, `diff` is huge for dim > 1024, but that case is unlikely. We could also use the efficient PyTorch formulation for fallback. But it's okay. In the chunk loop, `diff` shape is (chunk, N, dim). Since N=1024, chunk=512, diff is 64MB. This is fine. The output of `forward_svgd` is `out`, same shape as particles. What about the device? `particles` is on GPU. `dist_sq` is on GPU. `median` is computed on GPU. `h` is a Python float. Then passed to `forward_svgd`. In the C++ wrapper, `h` is passed as float. Good. Now, we should think about whether the chunked dist_sq in Python is the best approach or if we should use the PyTorch matmul. Let's consider the possibility that the tool output might show our kernel is not fast enough if the Python overhead is high. Chunked dist_sq in Python is 2 chunks * (unsqueeze + sub + pow + sum) = 2*4 = 8 PyTorch ops. Each op has kernel launch overhead (~5-10 us). Total overhead ~80 us. Plus compute ~0.5 ms. Plus median ~0.1 ms. Plus custom kernel ~0.1 ms. Total ~0.8 ms. Reference might take ~5 ms because of the 512MB allocation. So speedup ~6x. But what if reference is actually not that slow? To get even more speedup, we could compute dist_sq with a single custom kernel (tiled for coalescing) to reduce Python overhead. Let's see if we can write a simple custom dist_sq kernel with coalesced memory access. Coalesced dist_sq kernel design: - Have each block compute a tile of (i, j) pairs. - Use 2D block: (dim, BJ). For dim=32, BJ=32. - Each thread computes diff for one (i, j, d). - Then warp-shuffle reduce across d to get dist_sq. - Write result. This avoids loops and strided access. Let's write it. ``` __global__ void compute_dist_sq_kernel(const float* particles, int N, int dim, float* dist_sq) { int i = blockIdx.x; int tile_j = blockIdx.y; int d = threadIdx.x; int local_j = threadIdx.y; int BJ = blockDim.y; int j = tile_j * BJ + local_j; // load xi into shared memory extern __shared__ float s_xi[]; if (local_j == 0) { s_xi[d] = particles[i * dim + d]; } __syncthreads(); float xj = 0.0f; if (j < N) { xj = particles[j * dim + d]; } float diff = xj - s_xi[d]; float sq = diff * diff; // Reduce across d (warp shuffle) // Since blockDim.x = dim = 32, each warp is a column of local_j. for (int offset = dim / 2; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { sq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sq, offset); } if (d == 0 && j < N) { dist_sq[i * N + j] = sq; } } ``` Wait, `__shfl_down_sync` reduces within the warp. For block (32,32), the linear thread ID is `d + local_j * dim`. A warp is 32 consecutive threads. Since `dim=32`, `local_j` changes every 32 threads. So a warp consists of threads with the same `local_j` and `d=0..31`. So warp shuffle across d works perfectly. But if dim is not 32, this kernel needs to be adapted. For generality, we can assume dim <= 32 and blockDim.x = dim? But dim might be 16. Then a warp has 32 threads: 16 threads for local_j=0 (d=0..15), and 16 threads for local_j=1 (d=0..15). Then `__shfl_down_sync` across the warp would mix d values from different local_j. That's wrong. So this kernel only works if blockDim.x is exactly the warp size (32) or if dim = 32. To make it general, we need to ensure that each warp handles exactly one local_j. That means blockDim.x must be <= 32 and a divisor of 32? Actually the warp size is 32. Threads are ordered by x then y. For a 2D block (x, y), a warp of 32 threads will have x varying fastest. So if blockDim.x = dim, then a warp covers `dim` threads in x and `ceil(32/dim)` threads in y. For dim=32, it's exactly one local_j. For dim=16, a warp covers local_j=0..1 (16*2=32). Then warp shuffle would mix local_j values. To avoid that, we can set blockDim.x = 32 (warp size) and have each thread handle a subset of dimensions? Or we can loop over d with stride 32. But that defeats the purpose. Alternatively, we can set blockDim.x = 32 always, and each thread computes diff for a specific d mod 32? Not ideal. For simplicity, since benchmark dim=32, we can write the dist_sq kernel specialized for dim=32, or just use the generic 1D kernel with strided access, or PyTorch chunked. Let's stick with PyTorch chunked or matmul. It avoids writing a complex kernel. Actually, wait: can we compute dist_sq using a simple custom kernel but with better memory access by having each block handle one i and threads loop over j? Like: ``` __global__ void compute_dist_sq_kernel(const float* particles, int N, int dim, float* dist_sq) { int i = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int blockSize = blockDim.x; extern __shared__ float sdata[]; // sdata size = blockSize (for reduction) // Also s_xi for dimensions? We can store xi in registers per thread if each thread handles one d. // But if blockSize < dim, each thread handles multiple d. } ``` This is getting messy. Best approach: Use PyTorch matmul for dist_sq. It's the fastest and simplest. Let's assume it will be within tolerance. But to be safe, we can compute dist_sq using PyTorch's `torch.cdist` which computes pairwise distances in a stable and optimized way: ``` dist_sq = torch.cdist(particles, particles, p=2).pow(2) ``` `cdist` might use a block algorithm and be very fast. However, `cdist` computes sqrt of dist_sq and then we square it, which could introduce small errors. Also, for 1024x32, `cdist` is efficient. But the reference uses sum of squared differences. `cdist` might produce slightly different results due to algorithmic differences (e.g., using numerically stable accumulation). Tolerance is 1e-4, which is quite loose for fp32. So likely fine. Let's just use matmul: `dist_sq = norms + norms.t() - 2 * particles @ particles.t()`. Then clamp to non-negative: `dist_sq = dist_sq.clamp(min=0.0)`. This prevents negative values from causing NaN in sqrt or log, but here it's just exponential. Actually, we should `clamp(min=0.0)` because negative dist_sq due to rounding will cause `exp(-negative / (2h))` > 1, which is wrong. The reference never has negative dist_sq. So clamping is necessary for correctness. Let's do this in Python: ``` norms = (particles ** 2).sum(dim=1, keepdim=True) dist_sq = norms + norms.t() - 2.0 * (particles @ particles.t()) dist_sq = dist_sq.clamp(min=0.0) ``` This avoids any large intermediate. Then median, h, then custom kernel. Let's test this. But we need to be careful: the matmul approach might produce slight differences for the median. However, we can use the chunked diff approach to compute dist_sq exactly if needed. But matmul is faster. For the benchmark, speedup is important. Let's try matmul first. Actually, the chunked diff approach is also fast and exact. Let's compare: Matmul: - norms: reads particles (128KB), writes norms (4KB). - matmul: reads particles twice? Actually `particles @ particles.t()` reads particles (128KB) and the transposed view. It uses cuBLAS, highly optimized. Writes 4MB. - addition: reads norms (4KB), norms.t (4KB), prod (4MB), writes dist_sq (4MB). Total memory: small. Compute: matmul is ~33M ops. Very fast. Chunked diff: - chunk=512: diff is subtraction, reads 96KB, writes 64MB. square writes 64MB. sum writes 2MB. Times 2. Total memory: ~260MB. Compute: trivial. Launch overhead: 6 launches. Matmul is almost certainly faster. Let's use matmul. However, there is a potential issue: if particles has shape (N,dim), `particles @ particles.t()` creates a (N,N) tensor. The operation `norms + norms.t() - 2.0 * prod` will allocate a temporary for `2.0 * prod` (4MB) and for the subtraction/addition. But that's fine. PyTorch might fuse some of these with the JIT? Not necessarily. But even with temporaries, total memory is ~16MB. Very fast. Let's finalize with matmul for dist_sq. Wait, there is one more nuance: The reference computes `dist_sq = (diff ** 2).sum(-1)`. This is `sum_d (x_j[d] - x_i[d])^2`. The matmul computes `||x_i||^2 + ||x_j||^2 - 2 x_i·x_j`. For very close points, cancellation can cause slight negative values, which we clamp. The reference would produce exactly 0 for identical points (since diff=0). The matmul might produce a small negative value, which we clamp to 0. So for identical points, both give 0. Good. For points that are close but not identical, the cancellation might produce a small relative error. But squared distances are ~O(1-100). The dot product is O(30). The subtraction of two ~30 numbers to get a ~1 result can lose a few bits of precision. The relative error might be ~1e-6. That's well within tolerance. So matmul is fine. Let's finalize the code. We also want to make sure the C++ code compiles. `extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3"]`. Should we include `-std=c++17`? `load_inline` defaults to C++14 or C++17 depending on PyTorch. Adding `-O3` is fine. Let's write the final Python source. One more thought: Could we compute h inside the C++ wrapper by calling a CUDA reduction for median? No, use PyTorch median. Let's also check the `target_mean` buffer. It's not used, but we keep it. Another detail: In the reference, `score = self._score_fn(particles)` returns `-particles`. Our ModelNew doesn't need `_score_fn`. But it's okay to omit it. The task only requires same constructor args and forward signature. Let's write the code and compile it. I'll craft the string carefully. Actually, I want to consider whether `load_inline` can be called at module level. Yes, it compiles on import. We should wrap it in a try/except? No, that would be suspicious. But compilation errors will show up in `compile_kernel` output. Let's just write it. Also, I want to double-check the formula inside the kernel. Reference: `phi = (K @ score + grad_K.sum(dim=0)) / N`. `K @ score`: (N,N) @ (N,dim) => output row i = sum_j K_{i,j} * score_j. `grad_K.sum(dim=0)`: grad_K shape (N,N,dim). dim=0 sum => output row j = sum_i grad_K_{i,j}. So phi_i = (sum_j K_{i,j} * score_j + sum_j grad_K_{j,i}) / N? Wait earlier we concluded output of grad_K.sum(dim=0) is indexed by j (source). Let's re-evaluate with indices. In PyTorch: - `diff` shape (N,N,dim). diff[i,j] = x_j - x_i. - `K` shape (N,N). K[i,j] = exp(-dist_sq[i,j]/(2h)). - `grad_K` shape (N,N,dim). grad_K[i,j] = -(x_j - x_i)/h * K[i,j]. - `K @ score`: matrix multiplication of K (N,N) and score (N,dim). Result shape (N,dim). The row index of the result corresponds to the first index of K. So result[i,d] = sum_j K[i,j] * score[j,d]. - `grad_K.sum(dim=0)`: sum over first dimension (size N). Result shape (N,dim). The first dimension of the result corresponds to the second dimension of grad_K. So result[j,d] = sum_i grad_K[i,j,d]. - They add these two (N,dim) tensors and divide by N. So the output index is `i` for the first term and `j` for the second term? Wait, when adding two tensors of shape (N,dim), they are added elementwise. So output[k,d] = (K@score)[k,d] + (grad_K.sum(0))[k,d] for k = 0..N-1. (K@score)[k,d] = sum_j K[k,j] * score[j,d]. (grad_K.sum(0))[k,d] = sum_i grad_K[i,k,d]. So output[k] = (sum_j K[k,j] * score_j + sum_i grad_K[i,k]) / N. We earlier derived that sum_i grad_K[i,k] = sum_i (x_k - x_i)/h * K[i,k] = sum_j (x_k - x_j)/h * K[j,k]. Wait grad_K[i,k] = -(x_k - x_i)/h * K[i,k] = (x_i - x_k)/h * K[i,k]. So sum_i grad_K[i,k] = sum_i (x_i - x_k)/h * K[i,k] = (1/h) (sum_i x_i K[i,k] - x_k sum_i K[i,k]). So phi_k = (sum_j K[k,j] * score_j + sum_i (x_i - x_k)/h * K[i,k]) / N. Since K symmetric, K[i,k] = K[k,i]. So: phi_k = (sum_j K[k,j] * score_j + sum_j (x_j - x_k)/h * K[k,j]) / N. = (1/N) sum_j K[k,j] * (score_j + (x_j - x_k)/h). This matches our kernel formula: for output i (which is k), sum over j: term = K_{i,j} * (score_j + (x_j - x_i)/h). = K * (-x_j + (x_j - x_i)/h). So our kernel computes exactly this. Let's verify numerically with a small example to be absolutely sure. Let's write a small script mentally: x = [[1,2], [3,4]] (N=2, dim=2) score = [[-1,-2], [-3,-4]] diff[0,0] = [0,0], diff[0,1] = [2,2], diff[1,0] = [-2,-2], diff[1,1]=[0,0] dist_sq = [[0,8],[8,0]] K = [[1, e^{-4/h}], [e^{-4/h}, 1]] grad_K[0,1] = -[2,2]/h * K[0,1] = [-2/h, -2/h] * K01 grad_K[1,0] = -[-2,-2]/h * K[1,0] = [2/h, 2/h] * K01 grad_K.sum(dim=0) = [grad_K[0,0]+grad_K[1,0], grad_K[0,1]+grad_K[1,1]] = [[0,0] + [2/h,2/h]*K01, [-2/h,-2/h]*K01 + [0,0]] = [[2/h,2/h]*K01, [-2/h,-2/h]*K01] K @ score = [ [1*(-1,-2) + K01*(-3,-4)], [K01*(-1,-2) + 1*(-3,-4)] ] phi_0 = ( [-1,-2] + K01*[-3,-4] + [2/h,2/h]*K01 ) / 2 phi_1 = ( K01*[-1,-2] + [-3,-4] + [-2/h,-2/h]*K01 ) / 2 Now our kernel for i=0, j=0: diff = x0 - x0 = [0,0], K=1. term = 1 * (-x0 + 0/h) = [-1,-2]. j=1: diff = x1 - x0 = [2,2], K=K01. term = K01 * (-x1 + [2,2]/h) = K01 * ( [-3,-4] + [2/h, 2/h] ) = K01*[-3,-4] + K01*[2/h,2/h]. sum = [-1,-2] + K01*[-3,-4] + K01*[2/h,2/h]. -> matches phi_0 * 2? Wait phi_0 = sum / N = sum / 2. Yes, sum is 2*phi_0. Good. So kernel is correct. Great. Now, let's write the final code string and compile. Let me see: the joint distribution of x_j is isotropic Gaussian. For a fixed x_i, the density is exp(-||x_j||^2/2). Weighting by K(||x_j - x_i||^2) gives a biased sample. The mean of this biased distribution is some vector proportional to x_i, not zero. So E[ weighted x_j ] ≠ x_i * E[ weights ] in general, but for a Gaussian weight K, there might be a specific relation. Actually, K(x_j, x_i) = exp(-||x_j - x_i||^2 / (2h)). The product of two Gaussians: exp(-||x_j||^2/2) * exp(-||x_j - x_i||^2 / (2h)) ∝ exp(-( (1 + 1/h) ||x_j||^2 - 2 x_j·x_i / h + ||x_i||^2 / h ) / 2) ∝ exp(-(1 + 1/h)/2 * ||x_j - x_i/(1+h)||^2 + const) So the weighted distribution is Gaussian with mean x_i / (1 + h). Therefore: E[K_{ij} * x_j] / E[K_{ij}] = x_i / (1 + h) So for a Gaussian target, the weighted mean is NOT x_i but x_i / (1 + h). Thus: sum_j K_{ij} (x_j - x_i) ≈ N * E[K] * (x_i / (1 + h) - x_i) = -N * E[K] * h * x_i / (1 + h) ≠ 0! So the error term in my kernel would be proportional to x_i, which is not zero! E[K] is the average kernel value. For random points in 32D, most points are far away and K is small. The average K among all pairs might be somewhat small. Let me trace broadcasting more carefully. `particles.unsqueeze(0)` → shape (1, N, dim) `particles.unsqueeze(1)` → shape (N, 1, dim) In broadcasting, for `a - b`: - `a` has shape (1, N, dim), `b` has shape (N, 1, dim). - The result has shape (N, N, dim). - For result element at [i, j, d]: - a is read at index [0 (replicated), j, d] so a[j, d] (since the 1 in position 0 gets replicated i times) - b is read at index [i, 0 (replicated), d] so b[i, d] (since the 1 in position 1 gets replicated j times) Wait no. The broadcasting rules are: - Align dimensions from the right. - (1, N, dim) and (N, 1, dim) - dim matches. - N and 1: the 1 is broadcast to N. - 1 and N: the 1 is broadcast to N. So a (1, N, dim) is broadcast to (N, N, dim) by repeating the single row N times. b (N, 1, dim) is broadcast to (N, N, dim) by repeating the single column N times. For element [i, j, d] in the result: a contributes at [0, j, d] which is particles[j, d]. b contributes at [i, 0, d] which is particles[i, d]. diff[i, j] = a[j] - b[i] = x_j - x_i. So grad_K.sum(0)[i, d] = sum_j (x_j[d] - x_i[d]) / h * K[i, j] Then phi[i, d] = (1/N) [ sum_j K[i, j] * (-x_j[d]) + sum_j (x_j[d] - x_i[d]) / h * K[i, j] ] = (1/N) sum_j K[i, j] * [ -x_j[d] + (x_j[d] - x_i[d]) / h ] OH! grad_K.sum(0)[i, d] = sum_j (x_j[d] - x_i[d]) / h * K[i, j]!!! NOT (x_i[d] - x_j[d]) / h! Because grad_K[j, i] = -(diff[j, i]) / h * K[j, i] and diff[j, i] = x_i - x_j. So grad_K[j, i] = -(x_i - x_j) / h * K[j, i] = (x_j - x_i) / h * K[j, i]. And my kernel computes: term_j = K * (-x_j + (x_j - x_i) / h) So: my_kernel = (1/N) sum_j K[i, j] * [ -x_j + (x_j - x_i) / h ] ref = (1/N) sum_j K[i, j] * [ -x_j + (x_j - x_i) / h ] THEY ARE THE SAME!!!!!!!! I kept making sign errors in my mental math but now I see: diff[j, i] in the reference = x_i - x_j (because diff[j, i] = particles[i] - particles[j] from broadcasting). grad_K[j, i] = -diff[j, i] / h * K[j, i] = -(x_i - x_j) / h * K[j, i] = (x_j - x_i) / h * K[j, i]. And my kernel diff = xj - xi, so: term = K * (-x_j + (x_j - x_i) / h). This matches the reference exactly! So my kernel IS correct! Phew. The error of 4.84e-08 is just floating point differences (my dist_sq computation on CPU uses a different formula than the reference, and the kernel accumulates in a different order). Let's re-derive the reference carefully. Reference code: ```python score = self._score_fn(particles) # (N, dim), score = -particles diff = particles.unsqueeze(0) - particles.unsqueeze(1) # (N,N,dim) # diff[j,i] = particles[j] - particles[i] dist_sq = (diff ** 2).sum(-1) # (N,N) median_sq = torch.median(dist_sq.view(-1)).clamp(min=1e-5) h = median_sq / max(torch.log(torch.tensor(float(N))).item(), 1.0) K = torch.exp(-dist_sq / (2.0 * h)) # (N,N) grad_K = -diff / h * K.unsqueeze(-1) # (N,N,dim) # grad_K[j,i] = -(particles[j] - particles[i]) / h * K[j,i] phi = (K @ score + grad_K.sum(dim=0)) / N # phi[i] = (1/N) sum_j [ K[j,i] * score[j] + grad_K[j,i] ] # score[j] = -particles[j] # grad_K[j,i] = -(particles[j] - particles[i]) / h * K[j,i] ``` So the term inside the sum for dimension d is: `K[j,i] * (-particles[j,d]) + (-(particles[j,d] - particles[i,d]) / h * K[j,i])` `= K[j,i] * ( -particles[j,d] - (particles[j,d] - particles[i,d]) / h )` `= K[j,i] * ( -xj + (xi - xj)/h )` if diff = xj - xi. `= K[j,i] * ( -xj - (xj - xi)/h )` `= K[j,i] * ( -xj - diff/h )`. Let's check the broadcasting carefully. `particles.unsqueeze(0)` shape (1, N, dim). `particles.unsqueeze(1)` shape (N, 1, dim). `particles.unsqueeze(0) - particles.unsqueeze(1)`: This is `particles[0,j,:] - particles[i,0,:]`? This should speed up significantly. Also, the Python precomputation of dist_sq and median: ```python norms = (particles ** 2).sum(dim=1, keepdim=True) dist_sq = norms + norms.t() - 2.0 * (particles @ particles.t()) dist_sq = dist_sq.clamp(min=0.0) median_sq = dist_sq.view(-1).median().clamp(min=1e-5) h = median_sq.item() / max(math.log(float(N)), 1.0) ``` The `particles @ particles.t()` is a 1024x32 @ 32x1024 GEMM. This is quite small but in PyTorch it's highly optimized. The median operation on 1M elements might take some time. Can we compute the median in the kernel? That would avoid the GEMM and the `.median()` call on CPU/GPU sync. However, computing median on GPU is tricky. We could sort 1M floats. That could be done with `torch.sort`. But `torch.median` is already GPU optimized. The main cost is the GEMM which is O(N^2 dim) = 33M ops, same as the kernel itself. But the kernel also does 33M ops plus 1024^2 expfs. So the overall work is doubled. Can we compute h inside the kernel? We could compute all N^2 pairwise squared distances in registers (or shared memory), then sort them to find the median. But N=1024 so N^2=1,048,576. Sorting 1M floats on a single block is not feasible. But we could launch a separate kernel for it if we wanted. It might not be worth it. Actually, a better approach is to compute the pairwise distances in the kernel on the fly and do a reduction to find the median. But median of 1M numbers requires a sort. Thrust has sort but `load_inline` doesn't easily include thrust without complexity. We can use `torch.sort` on the distance matrix generated by the GEMM. But the GEMM generates the distance matrix already. Actually, the reference computes `dist_sq` from the large `(N, N, dim)` diff tensor, using `(diff ** 2).sum(-1)`. That's way more memory intensive. My Python precomputation using GEMM is already a huge optimization over the reference. So maybe my kernel is already fast enough. But let's optimize the kernel itself. Warp shuffle version: ```cpp __global__ void svgd_phi_kernel(const float* particles, float h, int N, int dim, float* out) { int i = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; float inv_h = 1.0f / h; float inv_2h = 0.5f * inv_h; float inv_N = 1.0f / (float)N; float xi = 0.0f; if (tid < dim) xi = particles[i * dim + tid]; float accum = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { float xj = 0.0f; if (tid < dim) xj = particles[j * dim + tid]; float diff = xj - xi; float sq = diff * diff; // warp reduce sq += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sq, 16); sq += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sq, 8); sq += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sq, 4); sq += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sq, 2); sq += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sq, 1); float dist_sq = sq; float K = expf(-dist_sq * inv_2h); if (tid < dim) { accum += K * (-xj + diff * inv_h); } } if (tid < dim) out[i * dim + tid] = accum * inv_N; } ``` We should ensure we use exactly one warp (32 threads). But `threadIdx.x` may exceed dim if dim < 32. We handle it with `if (tid < dim)`. Benchmark the current one vs this new one. But I can't benchmark directly, only correctness and final submit. However, I can run correctness. But actually, is there a way to make it even faster? Since N=1024, dim=32. We have 1024 blocks of 32 threads. The j loop is 1024 iterations. In each iteration, we do a warp shuffle reduction. The expf is the most expensive part. Maybe we can process two j's per iteration if we unroll? Not really. Wait, we can compute K once and use it for all dimensions? But each thread handles one dimension, and they all use the same K. In the current code, all threads in the warp compute K from the same `dist_sq`, which is correct because they broadcast via shuffle. Another optimization: loop unrolling. Since N=1024 is a power of two, we can use `#pragma unroll 4` or something. But modern compilers might unroll automatically. Alternatively, we can process multiple particles per block to increase arithmetic intensity and better utilize registers. For example, each block handles 8 i's (256 threads = 8 warps). Each warp handles one i. This might help with instruction-level parallelism as the SMs can interleave warps. But the total number of warps remains 32768. Occupancy is limited by the number of blocks, not threads per block. H100 has 132 SMs (I think). Each SM can have up to 32 blocks. With 1024 blocks, each SM gets ~7-8 blocks. Each block has 1 warp. So each SM has 7-8 warps out of 64 max warps per SM (max threads per SM 2048 / 32 = 64 warps). So occupancy is about 12.5%. If we make each block have 8 warps (256 threads), then 1024/8 = 128 blocks. Notice: `phi[i] = (1/N) sum_j K[i,j] * (-x_j + (x_j - x_i)/h)` `= (1/N) [ - sum_j K[i,j] x_j + (1/h) sum_j K[i,j] (x_j - x_i) ]` `= (1/N) [ - sum_j K[i,j] x_j + (1/h) (sum_j K[i,j] x_j - x_i * sum_j K[i,j]) ]` `= (1/N) [ -(1 + 1/h) * sum_j K[i,j] x_j + (x_i/h) * sum_j K[i,j] ]`. So for each i, we can compute: `sum_K = sum_j K[i,j]` `sum_Kx = sum_j K[i,j] * x_j` (vector) Then `phi[i] = (1/N) [ -(1 + 1/h) * sum_Kx + (x_i / h) * sum_K ]`. This requires computing K matrix first, then doing matrix-vector products. But computing K (N,N) = 1024x1024 is small. Then `sum_Kx` is a matrix multiplication `K^T @ particles` or `K @ particles` (since K is symmetric). We can compute K efficiently in the kernel or in PyTorch. From the derivation: `phi = (K @ score + grad_K.sum(dim=0)) / N` where `grad_K[j,i] = nabla_{x_i} K(x_i, x_j) = (x_j - x_i)/h * K[i,j]`. And `K @ score` row i = `sum_j K[i,j] * score[j] = sum_j K[i,j] * (-x_j)`. `grad_K.sum(dim=0)` row i = `sum_j grad_K[j,i] = sum_j (x_j - x_i)/h * K[i,j] = (1/h) (sum_j K[i,j] x_j - x_i sum_j K[i,j])`. So `phi[i] = (1/N) [ -sum_j K[i,j] x_j + (1/h)(sum_j K[i,j] x_j - x_i sum_j K[i,j]) ]`. `= (1/N) [ -(1 + 1/h) sum_j K[i,j] x_j + (x_i / h) sum_j K[i,j] ]`. Let `sum_K = K.sum(dim=1, keepdim=True)` shape (N,1). Let `sum_Kx = K @ particles` shape (N, dim). Then `phi = ( -(1 + 1/h) * sum_Kx + (particles / h) * sum_K ) / N`. This is purely matrix operations, no huge intermediate (N,N,dim) tensor. Steps: 1. `dist_sq = particles @ particles.t()` via GEMM. But we also need the diagonal to be 0. Actually: `norms = (particles ** 2).sum(dim=1, keepdim=True)` `dist_sq = norms + norms.t() - 2 * (particles @ particles.t())` This creates (N,N) tensor. With N=1024, that's 4MB. Acceptable. 2. `dist_sq = dist_sq.clamp(min=0.0)` (numerical stability). 3. `median_sq = dist_sq.view(-1).median().clamp(min=1e-5)`. 4. `h = median_sq / max(log(N), 1.0)`. 5. `K = exp(-dist_sq / (2.0 * h))`. 6. `sum_K = K.sum(dim=1, keepdim=True)`. 7. `sum_Kx = K @ particles`. 8. `phi = (-(1.0 + 1.0/h) * sum_Kx + (particles / h) * sum_K) / N`. This is very compact and uses highly optimized cuBLAS and elementwise kernels. Is it faster than the single custom kernel that loops over everything? So each thread computes: ```cpp int i = blockIdx.x; // the particle index int tid = threadIdx.x; int warp_id = tid / 32; int lane_id = tid % 32; float xi = (lane_id < dim) ? particles[i * dim + lane_id] : 0.0f; float accum = 0.0f; int j_start = warp_id * (N / num_warps); int j_end = (warp_id + 1) * (N / num_warps); for (int j = j_start; j < j_end; ++j) { float xj = (lane_id < dim) ? particles[j * dim + lane_id] : 0.0f; float diff = xj - xi; float sq = diff * diff; sq += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sq, 16); sq += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sq, 8); sq += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sq, 4); sq += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sq, 2); sq += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sq, 1); float K = expf(-sq * inv_2h); if (lane_id < dim) { accum += K * (-xj + diff * inv_h); } } ``` At this point, each lane_id in each warp has a partial sum `accum` for its dimension. Now we need to reduce across warps within the block. We can use shared memory: `__shared__ float sdata[8 * 32];` or just `__shared__ float sdata[256];`. Each thread writes its partial sum to `sdata[tid] = accum;`. Then we need to reduce for each lane_id across the 8 warps. That is, for lane_id d, sum over warp_id 0..7. One way: `__syncthreads();` then have warp 0 do the reduction: ```cpp __syncthreads(); if (warp_id == 0) { float val = sdata[lane_id]; // warp 0's own value for (int w = 1; w < num_warps; ++w) { val += sdata[w * 32 + lane_id]; } if (lane_id < dim) { out[i * dim + lane_id] = val * inv_N; } } ``` This uses only warp 0 for the final reduction, which is fine because there are only 8 values to sum. But wait, we need `sdata[w * 32 + lane_id]`. Each warp occupies 32 consecutive entries. This works. However, each block will now process j's across 8 warps in parallel. This means each block reads the entire particles matrix, but each warp reads a 128-particle chunk. The reads are still coalesced. Total reads from global memory: each warp reads 128 particles * 32 dims * 4 bytes = 16KB. Each block has 8 warps -> 128KB. 1024 blocks -> 128MB total, same as before. But because of L2 cache, it's fine. The difference is that now 8 warps per block are executing concurrently, increasing the number of active warps per SM. With 1024 blocks, each of 8 warps, total warps = 8192. This is close to the max resident warps (8448). So the GPU can keep almost all warps resident. Latency from expf can be perfectly hidden. But wait, can an SM hold a block with 8 warps? Yes, max 64 warps per SM. So each SM can hold up to 8 such blocks (8*8=64 warps). With 1024 blocks and 132 SMs, 1024/132 ≈ 7.8 blocks per SM. That's perfect! Each SM gets ~7-8 blocks, each with 8 warps, total ~60 warps per SM. Occupancy ~94%. This is excellent! This should significantly speed up the kernel. But there is a detail: the `for` loop inside each warp runs 128 iterations instead of 1024. But since there are 8 warps, total iterations per block is still 1024. The parallelism is across warps. With 8 warps per SM, when one warp stalls on expf, another warp can execute. This is the key. Wait, does each warp run its own independent loop? Yes. The warp scheduler within an SM switches between warps. So the new kernel should be much faster. One more optimization: instead of having warp 0 read from shared memory in a loop, we can have all warps participate in a tree reduction. But with only 8 values, it's fine. The `__syncthreads()` is needed before warp 0 reads. This is a one-time sync per block, much better than one per j iteration. Also, for `N` not divisible by num_warps, we need to handle remainders. Since N=1024 and num_warps=8, it divides evenly. Let's refine the kernel. We can make `num_warps` a template parameter or derive it from block size. For simplicity, we can hardcode block size 256 and 8 warps, but also pass N. The division `N / num_warps` should be exact. What about `dim`? In the benchmark, `dim=32`, which matches warp size. But to be general, if `dim > 32`, we would need multiple warps per i for dimension processing. But the problem says `dim=32` is benchmarked. We can add an assert or fallback. But perhaps we can write a general kernel that works for any dim by having each warp process `ceil(dim/32)` elements? For example, if dim=64, each warp handles 64 dimensions by iterating twice. But for simplicity, let's support `dim <= 32` with one warp per i, and `dim > 32` by falling back to PyTorch or a more general kernel. The problem says `dim=32` and `num_particles=1024`. Supporting exactly these sizes is sufficient. But to be safe, we can write a kernel that works for any `dim` as long as `dim <= 32` (which is the benchmark case). And if `dim > 32`, we use the PyTorch fallback. Wait, we need to make sure the kernel compiles. Let's write it. ```cpp __global__ void svgd_phi_kernel(const float* particles, float h, int N, int dim, float* out) { const int WARP_SIZE = 32; const int NUM_WARPS = 8; const int BLOCK_SIZE = WARP_SIZE * NUM_WARPS; // 256 int i = blockIdx.x; // each block handles one particle i if (i >= N) return; int tid = threadIdx.x; int warp_id = tid / WARP_SIZE; int lane_id = tid % WARP_SIZE; float inv_h = 1.0f / h; float inv_2h = 0.5f * inv_h; float inv_N = 1.0f / (float)N; float xi = 0.0f; if (lane_id < dim) { xi = particles[i * dim + lane_id]; } int j_per_warp = N / NUM_WARPS; int j_start = warp_id * j_per_warp; int j_end = j_start + j_per_warp; float accum = 0.0f; for (int j = j_start; j < j_end; ++j) { float xj = 0.0f; if (lane_id < dim) { xj = particles[j * dim + lane_id]; } float diff = xj - xi; float sq = diff * diff; // warp reduction sq += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sq, 16); sq += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sq, 8); sq += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sq, 4); sq += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sq, 2); sq += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sq, 1); float dist_sq = sq; float K = expf(-dist_sq * inv_2h); if (lane_id < dim) { accum += K * (-xj + diff * inv_h); } } // Store partial sum in shared memory __shared__ float sdata[BLOCK_SIZE]; sdata[tid] = accum; __syncthreads(); // Reduce across warps for each lane_id if (warp_id == 0 && lane_id < dim) { float val = sdata[lane_id]; for (int w = 1; w < NUM_WARPS; ++w) { val += sdata[w * WARP_SIZE + lane_id]; } out[i * dim + lane_id] = val * inv_N; } } ``` Grid: `N` blocks. Block: 256 threads. Wait, `sdata[lane_id]` is reading from thread `lane_id` (warp 0). This is fine because threads 0..31 wrote to sdata[0..31]. Then for `w=1`, we read sdata[32..63], etc. This works. But `i = blockIdx.x`. With `N=1024`, grid size 1024. `blockIdx.x` ranges 0..1023. Fine. What about `N` not divisible by `NUM_WARPS`? For `N=1024`, it's fine. Now, let's verify the formula once more. `phi[i] = (1/N) sum_j [K(x_i, x_j) * score(x_j) + nabla_{x_i} K(x_i, x_j)]`. `score(x_j) = -x_j`. `nabla_{x_i} K = (x_j - x_i)/h * K`. So term = `K * (-xj + (xj - xi)/h)`. In code: `K * (-xj + diff * inv_h)`. Correct. But wait, in the reference code: `diff = particles.unsqueeze(0) - particles.unsqueeze(1)` gives `diff[i,j] = particles[j] - particles[i]`. Then `grad_K = -diff / h * K.unsqueeze(-1)` which is `-(particles[j] - particles[i])/h * K`. Then `phi = (K @ score + grad_K.sum(dim=0)) / N`. Let's compute `grad_K.sum(dim=0)[i]` = `sum_j grad_K[j, i]`. `grad_K[j, i] = -(particles[i] - particles[j])/h * K[j,i] = (particles[j] - particles[i])/h * K[j,i]`. Since `K` is symmetric, `K[j,i] = K[i,j]`. So `grad_K.sum(dim=0)[i] = sum_j (particles[j] - particles[i])/h * K[i,j]`. This matches `nabla_{x_i} K` summed over j. Good. And `K @ score` row i = `sum_j K[i,j] * (-particles[j])`. So the total is `sum_j K[i,j] * (-particles[j]) + sum_j (particles[j] - particles[i])/h * K[i,j]`. This is `sum_j K[i,j] * ( -particles[j] + (particles[j] - particles[i])/h )`. Factor out K: `sum_j K * ( -xj + diff/h )`. This is exactly what my kernel computes: `accum += K * (-xj + diff * inv_h)`. j < j_end; ++j) {\n float xj = 0.0f;\n if (lane_id < dim) {\n xj = particles[j * dim + lane_id];\n }\n float diff = xj - xi;\n float sq = diff * diff;\n sq += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sq, 16);\n sq += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sq, 8);\n sq += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sq, 4);\n sq += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sq, 2);\n sq += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sq, 1);\n float dist_sq = sq;\n float K = expf(-dist_sq * inv_2h);\n if (lane_id < dim) {\n accum += K * (-xj + diff * inv_h);\n }\n }\n\n __shared__ float sdata[BLOCK_SIZE];\n sdata[tid] = accum;\n __syncthreads();\n\n if (warp_id == 0 && lane_id < dim) {\n float val = sdata[lane_id];\n #pragma unroll\n for (int w = 1; w < NUM_WARPS; ++w) {\n val += sdata[w * WARP_SIZE + lane_id];\n }\n out[i * dim + lane_id] = val * inv_N;\n }\n}\n\ntorch::Tensor forward_svgd(torch::Tensor particles, torch::Tensor h_tensor) {\n int N = particles.size(0);\n int dim = particles.size(1);\n auto out = torch::empty_like(particles);\n const int threads = 256;\n svgd_phi_kernel<<<N, threads>>>(\n particles.data_ptr<float>(), h_tensor.data_ptr<float>(), N, dim, out.data_ptr<float>());\n return out;\n}\n'''\n\nsvgd_cuda = load_inline(\n name=\"svgd_cuda\",\n cpp_sources=cpp_source,\n cuda_sources=cuda_source,\n functions=[\"forward_svgd\"],\n extra_cuda_cflags=[\"-O3\"],\n verbose=False,\n)\n\n\nclass ModelNew(nn.Module):\n \"\"\"SVGD update with custom CUDA kernel for the phi computation.\"\"\"\n\n def __init__(self, dim):\n super().__init__()\n self.dim = dim\n self.register_buffer(\"target_mean\", torch.zeros(dim))\n\n def _score_fn(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:\n return -x\n\n def forward(self, particles: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:\n N = particles.shape[0]\n dim = particles.shape[1]\n\n if dim > 32:\n score = -particles\n diff = particles.unsqueeze(0) - particles.unsqueeze(1)\n dist_sq = (diff ** 2).sum(-1)\n median_sq = dist_sq.view(-1).median().clamp(min=1e-5)\n h = median_sq.item() / max(math.log(float(N)), 1.0)\n K = torch.exp(-dist_sq / (2.0 * h))\n grad_K = -diff / h * K.unsqueeze(-1)\n phi = (K @ score + grad_K.sum(dim=0)) / N\n return phi\n\n norms = (particles ** 2).sum(dim=1, keepdim=True)\n dist_sq = norms + norms.t() - 2.0 * (particles @ particles.t())\n dist_sq = dist_sq.clamp(min=0.0)\n\n median_sq = dist_sq.median().clamp(min=1e-5)\n logN = torch.log(torch.tensor(float(N), device=particles.device))\n logN = logN.clamp(min=1.0)\n h_tensor = median_sq / logN\n\n return svgd_cuda.forward_svgd(particles, h_tensor)\n" }

import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import math
from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline

cpp_source = r'''
#include <torch/extension.h>
torch::Tensor forward_svgd(torch::Tensor particles, float h);
'''

cuda_source = r'''
#include <torch/extension.h>
#include <cuda_runtime.h>

__global__ void svgd_phi_kernel(const float* particles, float h, int N, int dim, float* out) {
    const int WARP_SIZE = 32;
    const int NUM_WARPS = 8;
    const int BLOCK_SIZE = WARP_SIZE * NUM_WARPS;

    int i = blockIdx.x;
    if (i >= N) return;

    int tid = threadIdx.x;
    int warp_id = tid / WARP_SIZE;
    int lane_id = tid % WARP_SIZE;

    float inv_h = 1.0f / h;
    float inv_2h = 0.5f * inv_h;
    float inv_N = 1.0f / (float)N;

    float xi = 0.0f;
    if (lane_id < dim) {
        xi = particles[i * dim + lane_id];
    }

    int j_per_warp = (N + NUM_WARPS - 1) / NUM_WARPS;
    int j_start = warp_id * j_per_warp;
    int j_end = j_start + j_per_warp;
    if (j_end > N) j_end = N;

    float accum = 0.0f;
    for (int j = j_start; j < j_end; ++j) {
        float xj = 0.0f;
        if (lane_id < dim) {
            xj = particles[j * dim + lane_id];
        }
        float diff = xj - xi;
        float sq = diff * diff;
        sq += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sq, 16);
        sq += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sq, 8);
        sq += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sq, 4);
        sq += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sq, 2);
        sq += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sq, 1);
        float dist_sq = sq;
        float K = expf(-dist_sq * inv_2h);
        if (lane_id < dim) {
            accum += K * (-xj + diff * inv_h);
        }
    }

    __shared__ float sdata[BLOCK_SIZE];
    sdata[tid] = accum;
    __syncthreads();

    if (warp_id == 0 && lane_id < dim) {
        float val = sdata[lane_id];
        #pragma unroll
        for (int w = 1; w < NUM_WARPS; ++w) {
            val += sdata[w * WARP_SIZE + lane_id];
        }
        out[i * dim + lane_id] = val * inv_N;
    }
}

torch::Tensor forward_svgd(torch::Tensor particles, float h) {
    int N = particles.size(0);
    int dim = particles.size(1);
    auto out = torch::empty_like(particles);
    const int threads = 256;
    svgd_phi_kernel<<<N, threads>>>(
        particles.data_ptr<float>(), h, N, dim, out.data_ptr<float>());
    return out;
}
'''

svgd_cuda = load_inline(
    name="svgd_cuda",
    cpp_sources=cpp_source,
    cuda_sources=cuda_source,
    functions=["forward_svgd"],
    extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3"],
    verbose=False,
)


class ModelNew(nn.Module):
    """SVGD update with custom CUDA kernel for the phi computation."""

    def __init__(self, dim):
        super().__init__()
        self.dim = dim
        self.register_buffer("target_mean", torch.zeros(dim))

    def _score_fn(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        return -x

    def forward(self, particles: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        N = particles.shape[0]
        dim = particles.shape[1]

        if dim > 32:
            score = self._score_fn(particles)
            diff = particles.unsqueeze(0) - particles.unsqueeze(1)
            dist_sq = (diff ** 2).sum(-1)
            median_sq = dist_sq.view(-1).median().clamp(min=1e-5)
            h = median_sq.item() / max(math.log(float(N)), 1.0)
            K = torch.exp(-dist_sq / (2.0 * h))
            grad_K = -diff / h * K.unsqueeze(-1)
            phi = (K @ score + grad_K.sum(dim=0)) / N
            return phi

        norms = (particles ** 2).sum(dim=1, keepdim=True)
        dist_sq = norms + norms.t() - 2.0 * (particles @ particles.t())
        dist_sq = dist_sq.clamp(min=0.0)

        median_sq = dist_sq.view(-1).median().clamp(min=1e-5)
        h = median_sq.item() / max(math.log(float(N)), 1.0)

        return svgd_cuda.forward_svgd(particles, h)
